            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

    <tr>
        
        <tr id="so2" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Year in Review: </b>The speedy Gardner managed to swipe 26 bases last season despite being granted just 248 at-bats. It shouldn't have come as a surprise. He was a stellar base runner in the minors and usually has a high rate of success when he attempts his thefts. He also made strides at the plate, posting a .337 wOBA. It was a vast and welcome improvement over his poor showing in 2008, when he finished with a .228/.283/.289 batting line and a .282 wOBA over 141 plate appearances. Still, Gardner is not in the batter's box enough to do anything truly special. He has made it clear that he can produce at a solid fantasy level when given adequate playing time, but the Yankees still seem to prefer Melky Cabrera in center field. <br /><br /><b>The Year Ahead: </b>Gardner averaged a .289/.389/.383 batting line during four seasons in the minor leagues and collected more than 50 stolen bases per 600 plate appearances. Regardless, he hasn't been given a fair shake in the bigs and probably won't anytime soon. The Yankees are content with using the 26-year-old as a utility outfielder, pinch-runner, and late-innings defensive replacement. He's an excellent fielder with outstanding speed and an above-average throwing arm, but the guy won't hold much fantasy value unless he's moved to another organization. Gardner's struggle for playing time won't end anytime soon and he's only worth drafting in AL-only leagues that allow daily roster movement. (Drew Silva)</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so3" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Gardner shined as an everyday player in 2010 by owning the zone and running wild on the bases. He might not fit the cookie-cutter image most people have of a corner outfielder, hitting majestic shots into the stands, but Gardner's plate discipline was sublime. The 27-year-old put up a .277/.383/.379 line with a .358 wOBA. Hacking at just 18.2% of pitches thrown out of the strike zone (third-lowest among qualified hitters), Gardner drew ball four nearly 14% of the time. Once on base, he swiped 47 bases in 56 attempts, good for an 84% success rate. Gardner has next to no power (.103 ISO in 2010), but he should remain an above-average hitter in 2011 thanks to his great eye and ability to beat out infield hits -- over the past two years, only Ichiro (another high-BABIP hitter) has a higher percentage of hits on ground balls. Last season represents the upper bound of his skill set, but Gardner will remain a great source of OBP and steals.  (David Golebiewski)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>A burner who has a clue at the plate, Gardner's good for forty-plus steals and an OBP north of .360. He's not a slugger, but playing Gardner beats the hell out of relying on some Willie Mays Hayes-type in a desperate attempt to boost your squad's running game. </td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so4" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>While the lamestream media may be constantly trying to find an upgrade for the Yankees in left field, the club is smart enough to know what they have in Gardner, so playing time should never be an issue for the 28-year-old. Gardner has a very narrow fantasy skillset, but his ability to get on base and make things happen in the context of a very good offense makes his reliable and valuable. His tendency to watch pitches fly by means he may never hit higher than .280, but owners should be willing to accept a lower average for huge run and stolen base numbers. (Zach Sanders)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Gardner is a machine when it comes to stealing bases, and his approach at the plate makes him a big-time asset in OBP leagues.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>An elbow injury that eventually required surgery limited Brett Gardner to only 16 games in 2012, but he’s set to return to action in 2013, and will likely start in the outfield for the Yankees. When he’s healthy, you know what you’re going to get with Gardner: he gets on base, and he makes things happen. Runs and stolen bases, that’s what Gardner’s all about, and there’s nothing wrong with that; in fact, it’s my opinion that the Brett Gardners of the baseball world ought to be more appreciated. Even if their fantasy value is mitigated by a lack of power and a so-so batting average. (Navin Vaswani)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Brett Gardner is set to return from an elbow injury in 2013, one that cost him all but 16 games in 2012. If he’s healthy, he’s worthy of fantasy consideration, thanks to the stolen bases and runs he’ll contribute to the Yankees lineup. On-base percentage matters, and Brett Gardner is living proof. Let nobody tell you otherwise.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>It's easy to be down on Brett Gardner. He hits a ton of infield flies and doesn't have great power, and strikes out a bit much, so he seems doomed to an iffy batting average. Last year he was healthy all season and only stole 24 bases. Pair that with eight homers and a wonky Yankees lineup, and that's how you get to be just barely worth a third outfielder spot in mixed leagues. And yet, he still managed to be worth double-digit dollars because steals were down across the league, and he was a non-zero in most categories. It's hard to project him for much better next year -- there's no stat that says he was unlucky, and his batting average on balls in play was actually a career high -- and yet that might be okay. At 30 years old, he's probably good for another year or two, and he might even steal more bases next year for the heck of it. He won't be needed for power as much, most likely, and maybe that means more attempts. Usually he's good for 40+ attempts in a full season. There's still some upside left, and besides batting average, there's no real reason to hate on Brett Gardner. (<a href="http://www.twitter.com/enosarris">Eno Sarris</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>A mediocre batting average, a handful of homers, and anywhere from 20 to 40 steals. Brett Gardner has been under-rated his whole life, he's comfortable turning that package into major league (and fantasy league) value.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Gardner carried over an approach at the plate in 2014 that had helped him post a sharp improvement in his isolated power the year before, hitting significantly more fly balls than he ever had before in his career and <a href="http://www.fangraphs.com/blogs/brett-gardners-just-doing-what-hes-been-dared-to-do/">being more aggressive</a> against pitches in the strike zone. Although the results didn’t manifest themselves in a big home run increase in 2013, they sure did last year, as his 17 long balls more than doubled what had been his career best. Much of the credit belongs to an 11.3% home run frequency on fly balls, which nearly doubled his 2013 total despite nearly the same average batted ball distance. Gardner’s improved pop wasn’t a Yankee Stadium-created phenomenon, either; his nine home runs on the road were more than what he managed at home. Unfortunately, the explosion in home run power came at the expense of other parts of Gardner’s game. His 21.1% strikeout rate was his highest over the course of a full season, and the increase in fly balls bit into his batted ball luck, though he still hits enough line drives and ground balls for owners to reasonably expect some improvement on the .256 average he posted. It’s also worth noting that he faded in the second half, as his weighted on-base average also plunged by nearly 40 points, though it’s possible some of that decline can be blamed on an <a href="http://nypost.com/2014/11/05/brett-gardner-recovering-from-core-muscle-surgery/">abdominal injury</a> which eventually required offseason surgery. Although he’s no longer the speed demon who averaged 48 steals between 2010 and 2011, at age 31, owners should be more than happy with him stealing more than 20 bags in each of the past two years, and he remains a prime source of runs as a key member of the Yankees offense. (<a href="https://twitter.com/Karl_de_Vries">Karl de Vries</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Gardner is at worst a solid third outfielder in standard mixed leagues, and while owners should anticipate some drop-off in the home run department, he helps in enough areas to be considered a safe pick among the top 30 outfielders.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Brett Gardner wasn’t necessarily involved in specific trades but his name has floated around a lot as a trade target after the Yankees added Aaron Hicks. Whether Gardner is on the Yankees or elsewhere, he’ll likely be hitting atop of the order. Gardner is no longer the 40+ steal threat that he was a few years ago and likely will top out at 20-25 steals, but he’s been able to pair that with homers in the teens of late. The projections call for more of the same with Gardner and there’s nothing to suggest otherwise. He’s no spring chicken anymore as he approaches his age 32 season but I would still expect Gardner to remain a top 30 outfielder in 2016. (Ben Pasinkoff)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>The only mistake one can really make here is expecting Gardner to anchor your stolen base category. While he’s a positive contributor to most categories, he’s no speed demon. A .260 15/20 season with a plus runs total is about what he’s done of late and he should be drafted accordingly.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>In a year where home runs spiked to steroid-era levels,Gardner's power output withered. The veteran left fielder managed just seven homers last season, marking his lowest full-season total since 2011. The steals continued to deteriorate, too. While Gardner still runs well, his days of 40+ steals are a distant memory; and at 33, his days of 20+ steals may be behind him as well. I'd also be wary of New York's crowded outfield situation, which could result in Gardner sitting against certain lefties.Gardner's low batting average and middling power/speed numbers give him minimal value in standard mixed leagues. He can still offer something in OBP leagues and deeper formats, but don't expect more than .260 with 25 homers + steals.<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Gardner's best years are behind him, but he can still get on base and give you double-digit homers and steals.Still, he's probably not worth the roster spot in a standard mixed league.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>
